import torch
import torch.nn as nn

class Model(nn.Module):
    """
    Pre-norm 1D dilated convolution block with BatchNorm: BatchNorm1d, ReLU activation, dilated Conv1d, and residual addition.
    At high dilation rates, memory access becomes non-contiguous across large strides in the sequence dimension.
    """
    def __init__(self, channels, kernel_size, dilation):
        super(Model, self).__init__()
        self.norm = nn.BatchNorm1d(channels)
        self.activation = nn.ReLU()
        self.conv = nn.Conv1d(channels, channels, kernel_size, padding=dilation, dilation=dilation)

    def forward(self, x):
        residual = x
        x = self.norm(x)
        x = self.activation(x)
        x = self.conv(x)
        x = x + residual
        return x

batch_size = 32
channels = 256
seq_len = 4096
kernel_size = 3
dilation = 128

def get_inputs():
    return [torch.randn(batch_size, channels, seq_len)]

def get_init_inputs():
    return [channels, kernel_size, dilation]

reasoning
We need to write an optimized CUDA kernel for a Conv1d with kernel_size=3, dilation=128, padding=dilation, plus BatchNorm1d, ReLU, and residual addition. It's a pre-norm residual block. The block computes: residual = x x = BatchNorm1d(x) x = ReLU(x) x = Conv1d(x) where Conv1d is in_channels=channels, out_channels=channels, kernel_size=3, padding=128, dilation=128, groups=1 (default) x = x + residual So, the output is residual + conv(relu(batch_norm(x))) We need to fuse these operations into a single CUDA kernel for speed. The dilation rate is high, so memory access is strided. The kernel should compute batch norm per channel: subtract running_mean (or training? BatchNorm1d in forward uses running statistics if not in training mode, but here it's presumably eval? Wait, the reference uses nn.BatchNorm1d which by default has training=False? Actually, in PyTorch, nn.BatchNorm1d forward uses training state from self.training. Since the model is not put in train mode, self.training=False by default. So it uses the running mean and var, and doesn't update them. The provided code doesn't set model.train() or model.eval(). Default is training=False. So BatchNorm1d will use running_mean, running_var, with momentum. Weight (gamma) and bias (beta) are also learned parameters. Thus the fused computation per element (after conv) is: Let x be input. For each c, we compute: x_norm = (x - running_mean[c]) / sqrt(running_var[c] + eps) y = weight[c] * x_norm + bias[c] Then z = max(y, 0) # ReLU Then conv: for each output position t, output[t] = sum_{k=0}^{2} weight_conv[c, k] * z[..., t + (k-1)*dilation]? Wait, kernel_size=3, padding=dilation. For standard Conv1d with kernel_size=3, padding=dilation, dilation=dilation: output length = floor((L + 2*padding - dilation*(kernel_size-1) - 1)/stride + 1. stride=1. L + 2*dilation - dilation*2 -1 +1 = L + 2*dilation - 2dilation = L. So output length is L. Good. The indices: For output position t (0-indexed), the conv accesses input positions: t - padding + k*dilation for k=0,1,2. Padding = dilation = 128. So positions: k=0: t - 128 k=1: t k=2: t + 128 But with padding, the padded input has dilation zeros on each side. So for t from 0 to L-1: - For k=0: index = t - 128. This is negative for t < 128, so it's zero (padding). - For k=1: index = t. This is always in range. - For k=2: index = t + 128. This is >= L for t >= L-128, so it's zero (padding). So effectively, for each channel c, the conv is: out[t] = w0 * z[t-128] + w1 * z[t] + w2 * z[t+128] where out-of-bounds terms are zero. Since the weight is per output channel and input channel, it's a full convolution: out[c_out, t] = sum_{c_in} w[c_out, c_in, k] * z[c_in, idx(t,k)] + bias_conv[c_out]. Wait, the conv is channels -> channels, groups=1. So weight shape is (channels, channels, 3). That's a lot of MACs: O(C^2 * L). With C=256, L=4096. C^2 * L * 3 = 256*256*4096*3 ≈ 805M multiplications-adds. That's heavy. The PyTorch reference likely uses cuDNN which is very optimized. However, since kernel_size=3 and dilation=128, it's extremely sparse in the time dimension but dense in channel dimension. cuDNN handles this well but maybe not perfectly. We can try to write a custom matmul-like kernel or exploit the sparsity. Wait, the total FLOPs: 256*256*4096*3*2 (mul+add) = about 1.6 GFLOPs. On a typical GPU, that's not huge but might be memory-bound because of the large weight size: 256*256*3 = 196,608 floats = 0.75 MB. Input is 32*256*4096 = 33.5M floats = 134 MB. Output similarly. So it's memory-bound because reading the input dominates. The weights can stay in cache. So our kernel's goal is to minimize memory traffic by fusing BN, ReLU, and conv into one pass over the input, writing only the final output. In PyTorch reference, the operations are separate: 1. BN: reads x (134MB), writes x (134MB) 2. ReLU: reads x, writes x (134MB) 3. Conv1d: reads x (134MB), reads weight (0.75MB), writes output (134MB) 4. Add residual: reads output (134MB), reads residual (134MB), writes output (134MB) Total memory traffic ~ 800MB reads + 400MB writes = 1200MB. At 1 TB/s memory bandwidth, that's about 1.2 ms. But the GPU has maybe 900 GB/s. So reference maybe ~1.3-2 ms. If we fuse everything into a single kernel: - Read x once (134MB) - Read weight (0.75MB) — can store in shared memory or registers. - Write output once (134MB). Total memory traffic ~ 270MB. That's about 4.5x reduction. So a fused kernel should be significantly faster. However, the challenge is the large weight matrix: 256x256x3. That's 196k floats. Shared memory per SM is typically 100KB or 164KB. So we cannot fit the full weight matrix in shared memory at once if using fp32 (each float 4 bytes => 786KB). But we can tile across channels. Since C=256, we can process a tile of output channels (e.g., 64) and a tile of input channels (e.g., 64). Weight tile size = 64*64*3 = 12,288 floats = 48KB. That fits in shared memory nicely. Input tile size = 64 * (some sequence length). But the sequence dimension is strided with dilation=128. We need to load input for the three taps. Alternatively, because the kernel is memory-bound and the weight matrix is relatively small, we can keep the weights in L2 cache and just load them from global memory. But to achieve high occupancy, we need enough warps. Since the compute is just a small matmul per position, we can use a thread block to compute a chunk of output channels for a chunk of time positions. Let's design the kernel. Data layout: NCHW for 1D conv: shape (N, C, L). In PyTorch, it's (N, C, L) contiguous in memory with outermost N, then C, then L. So memory layout is [n, c, l] -> n*C*L + c*L + l. Dilation 128 means that for a given output position t, we need indices t-128, t, t+128. Since padding=128, t-128 is aligned such that after padding the valid index mapping is: For k=0: if t >= 128, read z[n, c_in, t-128]. If t < 128, 0. For k=1: read z[n, c_in, t]. For k=2: if t < L-128, read z[n, c_in, t+128]. Else 0. So each output position only needs three input positions per channel. This is extremely sparse. The naive implementation would load all C channels for each of the three positions, which is 3*C loads per output element. Since the output has N*C_out*L elements (32*256*4096 ≈ 33.5M), and each output element requires a dot product over C_in=256 and 3 taps. So each output element requires loading 256*3 = 768 input values and 256*3 weight values. But the input values are reused across output channels. However, if we compute one output channel at a time, we need to load the entire input 3 times per output channel. That's huge memory traffic: 256*3*134MB = 103GB, which is bad. We need to tile over output channels to reuse the input values. Since the input values needed for a given time position t are the same for all output channels (just the three taps), we can load the input for a tile of time positions and a tile of input channels, and then compute a tile of output channels. This is similar to a GEMM but with sparse time taps. Because the sequence length is 4096, which is not huge, we could also precompute the ReLU+BN transformed input into shared memory or registers. But the input is 134MB, too large for shared memory. However, we can process one batch element at a time, and within that, process a tile of sequence positions. Goal: maximize reuse of input activations across output channels. Since C=256 is relatively small, maybe we can process all input channels in a block, and a group of output channels. For example, a block computes 64 output channels and all 256 input channels for a segment of L (say 64 time positions). For each time position t, the three taps are at t-128, t, t+128. These taps may be far apart in memory (128 floats apart). But within a block processing 64 consecutive t's, the taps for different t's are interleaved. Let's think about memory access pattern. Contiguous access is along the L dimension. If a warp loads x[c, t] for consecutive t, it's contiguous and coalesced. However, because of dilation, the taps are at t, t-128, t+128. For t in [0, 63], t-128 is negative (padding), so only t and t+128 are loaded. t and t+128 are 128 apart in memory for a fixed c. That's a strided load with stride 128 floats = 512 bytes. This is coalesced across threads in a warp if each thread loads a different t. For example, thread 0 loads x[c, t0], thread 1 loads x[c, t0+1], etc. For tap0 (t-128), thread i loads x[c, t0+i-128]. Still contiguous across threads. Similarly tap2 (t+128): thread i loads x[c, t0+i+128], also contiguous. So each tap is a contiguous vector of length block_len. We can use float4 or vectorized loads for each tap. Thus, we can vectorize loads per channel and per tap. But we have 256 channels. So for each tap, we need to load all 256 channels for a set of time positions. If we process all 256 input channels inside the block, we can load channel by channel. Alternatively, we can transpose the data mentally: we have an input of shape (N, C, L). We want to compute for each (n, c_out, t) the sum over c_in and k of W[c_out, c_in, k] * X[n, c_in, idx(t,k)]. This is essentially a 1x1 convolution for each tap, summed. Because the taps are independent in channel mixing. So we can think of it as: out = W[:, :, 0] * X_shifted(-128) + W[:, :, 1] * X + W[:, :, 2] * X_shifted(+128) where W[:, :, k] is a matrix of shape (C_out, C_in), and X_shifted is the input shifted in time. If we treat each tap as a matrix-vector product per time position. So for each time t, we need to compute: out_t = M0 * x_{t-128} + M1 * x_t + M2 * x_{t+128} where x_t is a vector of length C_in, and M_k are C_out x C_in matrices. This is like three GEMVs per time position (or a GEMM if we batch time positions). Since C_in = C_out = 256, each GEMV is 256x256. For 4096 time positions, it's 4096 * 3 * (256*256) = 805M MACs. That's a lot. But if we batch 64 time positions together, we get a GEMM of size (C_out, C_in) multiplied by (C_in, batch_len) for each tap. So for each tap, it's a 256x256 * 256x64 GEMM. Total 3 * 256*256*64 = 12.5M MACs per batch of 64 time positions. With 4096/64=64 batches. So 800M MACs total. NVIDIA's cuBLAS can do this very fast, but we have shifts and padding, plus BN and ReLU fused. However, cuBLAS might not easily fuse the nonlinearity and the shifts. But we could call three cuBLAS GEMMs for the three taps and sum them? That would involve reading the weight matrices three times (or same weight? Actually W for each tap is different, so three weight matrices). But BN+ReLU would need to be applied to X first. So we could precompute Z = BN(ReLU(X)) into a buffer, then call three GEMMs? But GEMM requires contiguous data for the batch dimension. Shifting is just adjusting pointers. For tap0, we can treat the input as Z[:, :, 0:L-128] (if we ignore padding for a moment). For tap1, Z[:, :, 128:L+128]? Actually, with padding, we need to handle boundaries. But the simplest high-performance approach is to write a custom kernel that does the GEMM-like computation in registers/shared memory, fused with BN+ReLU and residual add. However, writing a fast 256x256 GEMM inside a kernel is non-trivial but manageable because the dimensions are small. In fact, for each output channel tile and input channel tile, we can compute the partial sum in registers. Let's estimate performance. Memory bandwidth is the bottleneck. A single fused kernel reading input and weight and writing output is ~270MB. At 900 GB/s, that's ~0.3 ms. But the compute might add overhead. The reference might be ~1-2 ms. So a 2-5x speedup is possible. Thus, the kernel design: Grid: (batch_size, num_tiles_t, num_tiles_c_out) = (32, 128, 2) because C_out=256, TILE_C_OUT=128. Block: (32, 8) = 256 threads. TILE_L = 32. TILE_C_IN = 16. TILE_C_OUT = 128. Loop over in_tiles = 0..15. In each iteration: 1. Load x into x_shared[16][3][32] (6KB). Each thread loads 6 floats (1536/256=6). For idx = thread_id; idx < 16*3*32; idx += 256: ic = idx / (3*32) rem = idx % (3*32) k = rem / 32 t = rem % 32 global_t = block_t_start + t + (k-1)*dilation if global_t < 0 or >= seq_len: val = 0 else: gc = in_tile_start + ic val = x[n, gc, global_t] // BN val = (val - mean[gc]) * inv_std[gc] val = val * gamma[gc] + beta[gc] val = fmaxf(val, 0.0f) x_shared[ic][k][t] = val; 2. Sync. 3. Compute: tx = threadIdx.x; // 0..31 -> time offset ty = threadIdx.y; // 0..7 -> oc group oc_base = block_c_out_start + ty*16; t_global = block_t_start + tx; float acc[16] = {0}; for ic=0..15: float x0 = x_shared[ic][0][tx]; float x1 = x_shared[ic][1][tx]; float x2 = x_shared[ic][2][tx]; for i=0..15: int oc = oc_base + i; float w0 = weight[oc][in_tile_start + ic][0]; float w1 = weight[oc][in_tile_start + ic][1]; float w2 = weight[oc][in_tile_start + ic][2]; acc[i] += w0*x0 + w1*x1 + w2*x2; // Note: all threads in a warp load the same weight, broadcast from global memory. 4. After all in_tiles, add conv bias, add residual, write output. for i=0..15: int oc = oc_base + i; float bias_val = conv_bias[oc]; float res = x_input[n, oc, t_global]; float out = acc[i] + bias_val + res; y[n, oc, t_global] = out; But wait: the conv output before adding residual is x = self.conv(x). The bias of conv is included in the conv output. So we add conv_bias. Then x = x + residual. So yes, out = acc + bias + residual. Now, we need to ensure correctness. The padding is dilation=128. So the conv formula for dilation conv1d with padding=128 and kernel_size=3: out[t] = sum_{c_in} sum_{k=0}^{2} W[c_out, c_in, k] * input[c_in, t + (k-1)*dilation] where input is the padded input. With padding=128 on both sides, the padded input has length L + 2*128. The valid output positions t=0..L-1 correspond to reading padded input at indices: pad_idx = t + 128 + (k-1)*128 = t + k*128. So for k=0: t + 0 -> left padding? Wait, let's derive carefully. Standard Conv1d formula: out[n, c_out, t] = bias[c_out] + sum_{c_in} sum_{k=0}^{K-1} W[c_out, c_in, k] * padded_input[n, c_in, t * stride + k * dilation - pad] Here stride=1, pad=128, dilation=128. So out[n, c_out, t] = bias[c_out] + sum_{c_in} sum_{k=0}^{2} W[c_out, c_in, k] * padded_input[n, c_in, t + k*128 - 128]. Plug in k=0: padded_input[t - 128]. Since padded_input has 128 zeros at the start, for t < 128, this is 0. For t >= 128, it's input[t-128]. k=1: padded_input[t]. This is input[t] for all t. k=2: padded_input[t + 128]. For t < L-128, it's input[t+128]. For t >= L-128, it's 0 (right padding). So our earlier mapping global_t = block_t_start + t_local + (k-1)*dilation is correct: - k=0 => global_t = block_t_start + t_local - 128. - k=1 => global_t = block_t_start + t_local. - k=2 => global_t = block_t_start + t_local + 128. And we check bounds against [0, seq_len). If out of bounds, val=0. That's correct. Now, one important detail: the BatchNorm parameters. Now, the kernel code. Important: The dilated conv with padding=128 means that for t near boundaries, some taps are zero. We handle this in the load. Also, we need to add the residual. The residual is the original input `x` at (n, c_out, t). Note: `x` is the original input, shape (N, C, L). So `res = x[n*channels*seq_len + oc*seq_len + t]`. Now, about the padding: The conv uses `padding=dilation`. Our kernel handles it by loading zero when out of bounds. That's correct. Let's code the kernel. We also need to consider that the weight order in PyTorch is (out_channels, in_channels, kernel_size). In CUDA, we can access it linearly using indices. Weight index: ```cpp int w_stride_oc = channels * kernel_size; // since in_channels = channels int w_stride_ic = kernel_size; // W[oc][ic][k] = weight[oc * w_stride_oc + ic * w_stride_ic + k] ``` But note: in the kernel, `channels` is both in_channels and out_channels. Since it's the same, we can use a single variable. Input index: ```cpp int x_stride_n = channels * seq_len; int x_stride_c = seq_len; // x[n][c][t] = x[n * x_stride_n + c * x_stride_c + t] ``` Similarly for output: ```cpp int out_stride_n = channels * seq_len; int out_stride_c = seq_len; ``` Now, writing the kernel. ```cpp __global__ void fused_dilated_conv_kernel( const float* __restrict__ x, const float* __restrict__ bn_mean, const float* __restrict__ bn_invstd, const float* __restrict__ bn_weight, const float* __restrict__ bn_bias, const float* __restrict__ conv_weight, const float* __restrict__ conv_bias, float* __restrict__ out, int batch_size, int channels, int seq_len, int dilation ) { constexpr int TILE_L = 32; constexpr int TILE_C_IN = 16; constexpr int TILE_C_OUT = 128; // Grid: (batch, tiles_l, tiles_c_out) int n = blockIdx.x; int tile_l = blockIdx.y; int tile_c_out = blockIdx.z; int t_start = tile_l * TILE_L; int c_out_start = tile_c_out * TILE_C_OUT; int tx = threadIdx.x; // 0..31 -> time within tile int ty = threadIdx.y; // 0..7 -> oc group int t = t_start + tx; // Check bounds if (t >= seq_len) return; // but seq_len is multiple of TILE_L, so not needed int oc_base = c_out_start + ty * (TILE_C_OUT / blockDim.y); // blockDim.y=8, so 16 // oc_base = c_out_start + ty * 16 // Shared memory for transformed input __shared__ float x_shared[TILE_C_IN][3][TILE_L]; float acc[16]; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < 16; ++i) acc[i] = 0.0f; int num_in_tiles = channels / TILE_C_IN; int x_stride_n = channels * seq_len; int x_stride_c = seq_len; int w_stride_oc = channels * 3; // kernel_size=3 int w_stride_ic = 3; for (int tile_c_in = 0; tile_c_in < num_in_tiles; ++tile_c_in) { int c_in_start = tile_c_in * TILE_C_IN; // Load input into shared memory // Total loads: TILE_C_IN * 3 * TILE_L = 1536 int tid = threadIdx.y * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; // 0..255 int total_loads = TILE_C_IN * 3 * TILE_L; for (int idx = tid; idx < total_loads; idx += blockDim.x * blockDim.y) { int ic = idx / (3 * TILE_L); int rem = idx % (3 * TILE_L); int k = rem / TILE_L; int t_local = rem % TILE_L; int global_t = t_start + t_local + (k - 1) * dilation; float val; if (global_t < 0 || global_t >= seq_len) { val = 0.0f; } else { int c = c_in_start + ic; int x_idx = n * x_stride_n + c * x_stride_c + global_t; val = x[x_idx]; // BatchNorm + ReLU float mean = bn_mean[c]; float invstd = bn_invstd[c]; float w_bn = bn_weight[c]; float b_bn = bn_bias[c]; val = (val - mean) * invstd; val = val * w_bn + b_bn; val = fmaxf(val, 0.0f); } x_shared[ic][k][t_local] = val; } __syncthreads(); // Compute int c_in = c_in_start; // global ic start // Each thread processes time tx and oc_base + i // Load x for this thread's tx float x0 = x_shared[0][0][tx]; float x1 = x_shared[0][1][tx]; float x2 = x_shared[0][2][tx]; #pragma unroll for (int ic = 0; ic < TILE_C_IN; ++ic) { // Load input for this ic and tx // We can load ahead to reduce stalls? // For simplicity, load now. float xv0 = x_shared[ic][0][tx]; float xv1 = x_shared[ic][1][tx]; float xv2 = x_shared[ic][2][tx]; int global_ic = c_in_start + ic; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < 16; ++i) { int oc = oc_base + i; // Load conv weights (broadcast across warp) float w0 = conv_weight[oc * w_stride_oc + global_ic * w_stride_ic + 0]; float w1 = conv_weight[oc * w_stride_oc + global_ic * w_stride_ic + 1]; float w2 = conv_weight[oc * w_stride_oc + global_ic * w_stride_ic + 2]; acc[i] += w0 * xv0 + w1 * xv1 + w2 * xv2; } } __syncthreads(); } // Write output int oc_base_val = oc_base; int out_stride_n = channels * seq_len; int out_stride_c = seq_len; // Load residual and add bias #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < 16; ++i) { int oc = oc_base_val + i; if (oc < channels) { int out_idx = n * out_stride_n + oc * out_stride_c + t; float res = x[n * x_stride_n + oc * x_stride_c + t]; float bias = conv_bias[oc]; float val = acc[i] + bias + res; out[out_idx] = val; } } } ``` Wait, `oc_base` might exceed channels if channels not divisible by 128. But channels=256, so ok. But there is a major issue: the loop `for (int ic = 0; ic < TILE_C_IN; ++ic)` uses `x_shared[ic]...`. However, in the preceding lines, I had `float x0 = x_shared[0][0][tx];` outside the loop, which is unused. The loop loads `x_shared[ic]...`. That's fine. But I need to be careful about register spilling. The loop over `i = 0..15` with 3 weight loads and 3 FMAs per iteration. The compiler will unroll this. The weights are loaded from global memory with broadcast. The `acc` array of 16 floats is stored in registers. That's fine. Now, we need the host wrapper for pybind11. `load_inline` generates pybind11 binding for functions listed in `functions=[...]`. The wrapper should take torch tensors and extract pointers. Wait, we need to define a function that takes `at::Tensor` arguments and calls the kernel. Since `load_inline` auto-generates the pybind11 wrapper, we just write a C++ function. Example: ```cpp torch::Tensor fused_forward( torch::Tensor x, torch::Tensor bn_mean, torch::Tensor bn_invstd, torch::Tensor bn_weight, torch::Tensor bn_bias, torch::Tensor conv_weight, torch::Tensor conv_bias, int dilation ) { // checks int batch_size = x.size(0); int channels = x.size(1); int seq_len = x.size(2); auto out = torch::empty_like(x); dim3 block(32, 8); dim3 grid(batch_size, (seq_len + 31) / 32, (channels + 127) / 128); fused_dilated_conv_kernel<<<grid, block>>>( x.data_ptr<float>(), bn_mean.data_ptr<float>(), bn_invstd.data_ptr<float>(), bn_weight.data_ptr<float>(), bn_bias.data_ptr<float>(), conv_weight.data_ptr<float>(), conv_bias.data_ptr<float>(), out.data_ptr<float>(), batch_size, channels, seq_len, dilation ); return out; } ``` But we need to ensure that x is contiguous and on CUDA. We can add checks or just assume. Also, note that the weight order for Conv1d is (out_channels, in_channels, kernel_size). In the kernel, we assumed this. Wait, standard PyTorch Conv1d with `padding=dilation` and `dilation=dilation` will pad `dilation` on each side, regardless of kernel_size. The formula for output length is (L + 2*padding - dilation*(kernel_size-1) - 1)/stride + 1. If padding=dilation, this equals L + 2*dilation - dilation*(kernel_size-1). For output length to equal L, we need 2*dilation = dilation*(kernel_size-1) => kernel_size-1 = 2 => kernel_size=3. So with kernel_size=3 and padding=dilation, output length = L. They are constants. - We can precompute the residual load in the write phase. One subtlety: The weight access pattern `conv_weight[oc * w_stride_oc + global_ic * w_stride_ic + k]` uses `global_ic` which is inside the `ic` loop. The address changes by `w_stride_ic = 3` per `ic` iteration. Wait, `global_ic` increments by 1 each `ic` iteration. So weight address increments by `w_stride_ic = 3` floats. That's contiguous. `oc` changes in the inner `i` loop, which increments by 1 and address increments by `w_stride_oc = channels*3 = 768` floats. This is a stride of 768*4 = 3072 bytes. In global memory, this is strided. Let's design `ModelNew`: ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline # Define the CUDA source as a string cuda_source = r''' #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> __global__ void fused_dilated_conv_kernel(...) { ... } torch::Tensor fused_forward( torch::Tensor x, torch::Tensor bn_mean, torch::Tensor bn_invstd, torch::Tensor bn_weight, torch::Tensor bn_bias, torch::Tensor conv_weight, torch::Tensor conv_bias, int dilation ) { // ... } ''' class ModelNew(nn.Module): def __init__(self, channels, kernel_size, dilation): super().__init__() assert kernel_size == 3, "Only kernel_size=3 is supported" self.channels = channels self.kernel_size = kernel_size self.dilation = dilation # Use PyTorch modules for parameter initialization and state dict self.norm = nn.BatchNorm1d(channels) self.activation = nn.ReLU() self.conv = nn.Conv1d(channels, channels, kernel_size, padding=dilation, dilation=dilation) # Precompute invstd # But running_var might change? In eval mode, it's fixed. # We can compute it in forward. def forward(self, x): # Ensure x is contiguous and on CUDA x = x.contiguous() # Compute invstd eps = self.norm.eps # running_var is guaranteed to be positive invstd = 1.0 / torch.sqrt(self.norm.running_var + eps) # Call custom kernel out = fused_forward( x, self.norm.running_mean, invstd, self.norm.weight, self.norm.bias, self.conv.weight, self.conv.bias, self.dilation ) return out ``` We need to define `fused_forward` as a `cpp_sources` function and pass it in `functions=['fused_forward']`. But we need to include `<torch/extension.h>`. Also, we need to handle the case where `x` is not on CUDA. We can add a guard in the wrapper: if not `x.is_cuda()`, maybe fallback to reference? No, we should not fallback. We can just assert `x.is_cuda()`. Also, we should ensure `x` is `float` (fp32). The tolerance is fp32. Let's think about potential correctness issues. 1. BatchNorm: In the kernel, we compute `val = (val - mean) * invstd; val = val * weight + bias; val = max(val, 0)`. That's correct for eval mode. 2. BN uses `self.norm.eps`. In PyTorch default eps is 1e-5. We need to use the same. We can pass eps to the kernel or precompute invstd with eps. Precomputing invstd on the host with `self.norm.running_var + self.norm.eps` is correct. 3. ReLU: `fmaxf(val, 0.0f)`. Correct. 4. Conv: The weight order. In PyTorch, `conv.weight` shape is (out_channels, in_channels, kernel_size). The kernel expects `conv_weight[oc * (channels*3) + ic * 3 + k]`. That matches. 5. Padding: We set `val=0` for out-of-bounds. Since padding=dilation and we only access `t + (k-1)*dilation`, this matches the padded input. 6. Residual: We add `x[n, oc, t]` (original input). Correct. Let me double-check the load_inline call signature. `torch.utils.cpp_extension.load_inline(name, cpp_sources, cuda_sources=None, functions=None, extra_cflags=None, extra_cuda_cflags=None, extra_ldflags=None, extra_include_paths=None, build_directory=None, is_python_module=True, verbose=False, with_cuda=None)` returns the loaded module if `is_python_module=True`. So: ```python _cuda_ext = load_inline( name='fused_dilated_conv', cpp_sources='', cuda_sources=cuda_source, functions=['fused_forward'], extra_cuda_cflags=['-O3'], verbose=False ) fused_cuda_forward = _cuda_ext.fused_forward ``` Then in forward: `return fused_cuda_forward(...)` Now, let's refine the kernel. One issue: The kernel loads `bn_mean`, `bn_invstd`, etc. from global memory inside the `for (int idx = tid; idx < total_loads; idx += blockDim.x*blockDim.y)` loop. The parameters are small (256 floats). They will be in L1 constant cache or L2. Accessing them inside the loop is fine. But we can make them `__restrict__` and they are already pointers. Another issue: In the compute loop, we have `float w0 = conv_weight[...];`. Since `oc` is constant across the warp, the compiler might keep it in a register and reuse. We can add `const float` but it doesn't matter. The inner loop over `i` (0..15) computes `acc[i] += w0*xv0 + w1*xv1 + w2*xv2;`. We can rewrite as: ```cpp float prod0 = w0 * xv0; float prod1 = w1 * xv1; float prod2 = w2 * xv2; acc[i] += prod0 + prod1 + prod2; ``` This might reduce register pressure? Or the compiler will do FMAs. Actually `acc += w0*xv0` is an FMA. Then `acc += w1*xv1` is another FMA. Then `acc += w2*xv2` is a third. That's 3 FMAs per i. Since there are 16 i's, that's 48 FMAs per ic. 16 ic's = 768 FMAs per thread. This is a decent amount of compute per block. Memory loads are small. So compute-to-memory ratio is high enough. One potential issue: The `acc` array is 16 floats. The compiler may allocate 16 registers. The loop over `i` may unroll. With `#pragma unroll`, the compiler will fully unroll the inner loop if possible. That's 48 FMAs. The SMs have a lot of FMA throughput (e.g., A100 has 256 FP32 FMAs per clock per SM). With 256 threads (8 warps), 768 FMAs per thread = 196,608 FMAs per block. At 1.4GHz, FP32 throughput is ~350 TFLOPS? Actually 19.5 TFLOPS for A100. So 196k FMAs is tiny. The block should finish quickly. But the kernel may be latency-bound by the global memory loads of weights (even from L1). However, since the weights are broadcast and in L1, latency is low. And there is enough ILP from the 16 accumulators to hide latency. Now, let's think about the residual addition. In the kernel, for each output, we load `x[n * x_stride_n + oc * x_stride_c + t]`. This is reading the original input. However, the original input is also being read in the first loop for BN, but for different channels (input channels). For output channels, we read them only at the end. The original input is 134MB. The writes are 134MB. So the memory traffic is roughly reading half the input for BN (since each input element is read by ~3 blocks? Wait, we optimized that by processing 128 output channels per block, so each input element is read 3 times (for 3 taps). Actually, we still read the input for BN in each block. With 128 output channels per block, the number of blocks reading a given input element is 3 (one per tap). So total input reads = N*C*L*3 floats = 402MB. Plus residual reads = N*C*L floats = 134MB. So total reads = 536MB. Writes = 134MB. Total memory = 670MB. At 900GB/s, that's ~0.74ms. The PyTorch reference has ~1.2GB memory traffic (BN + ReLU + Conv + Add). So we expect about 1.5-2x speedup. The compute is negligible. But can we do even better? The residual read could be avoided if we compute the residual addition in a separate kernel or if we fuse it? Wait, we are already fusing the residual addition. The residual read is necessary. Total reads: 3x for input (because of 3 taps) + 1x for residual = 4x the input size. This is inherent to the operation (dilated conv with 3 taps reads 3x the input, plus residual read). So we cannot reduce below 4x input reads + 1x output write. That's 5*134MB = 670MB. So our fused kernel is near-optimal in memory traffic. But wait: The input for the conv is the BN-transformed input. The original input is needed for the residual. So we must read the original input at least twice: once for BN (producing transformed input) and once for residual. Then the conv reads the transformed input via 3 taps. However, if we fuse BN into the conv load, we read original input 3 times (for taps) and 1 time for residual. That's 4 reads. If BN were separate, we would read input once, write transformed input, then conv reads transformed input 3 times, then add reads original input once and conv output once, writing output. That's 1 read + 1 write (BN) + 3 reads (conv) + 2 reads + 1 write (add) = 5 reads + 2 writes = 7 memory passes. Our fused kernel does 4 reads + 1 write = 5 passes. So we save 2 passes. Good. Could we avoid reading the original input for residual by storing it in shared memory? No, because the block processes output channels that may not coincide with input channels. But we process 128 output channels per block. For a given block, we need residual for those 128 channels and 32 time positions. That's 128*32 = 4096 floats = 16KB. We could load the residual tile into shared memory at the end? But we still need to read it from global memory. It's the same traffic. Alternatively, we could load the residual tile at the beginning (since it's the original input for output channels). But the original input for output channels is separate from the input channels we load for BN. So we would load an extra 16KB per block. Not a big deal. But we still read it. Could we compute BN and store transformed input in registers and also keep original input in registers for the residual? We process output channels, and the residual is for the same output channels. When we write output, we add the original input. That's fine. Now, there is a possible issue with using `blockIdx.x` for batch. The grid is `dim3 grid(batch_size, num_tiles_l, num_tiles_c_out)`. That's correct. Now, let's think about numerical precision. The operations order in our kernel is: acc += w * ((x - mean) * invstd * gamma + beta) for each tap. In PyTorch, it computes BN in float32, ReLU in float32, Conv in float32 (using cuDNN which might use TF32 or other algorithms, but for FP32 it does FP32). The accumulation order might differ slightly (e.g., tree reduction vs loop). Our kernel accumulates in the order: for each ic, for each i (oc), for each k, multiply-add. This is a specific order. The reference might use a different order (e.g., GEMM). The tolerance is 1e-4 atol. So slight differences should be fine. But we need to be careful about the `fmaxf`. In PyTorch, `ReLU` returns `max(x, 0)`. If x is exactly zero, both return zero. If x is negative, both return zero. `fmaxf` uses CUDA intrinsic. reasoning
The compile failed because the `cpp_sources` was empty, meaning the CUDA source was probably treated as main.cpp which doesn't help locate the function. Actually, load_inline auto-generates a pybind module from `functions`. The cuda_sources probably goes into `cuda.cu`, but the generated C++ wrapper using `torch::wrap_pybind_function` isn't finding the function declaration because the generated main.cpp doesn't know about it. We need to provide a `cpp_sources` small wrapper or at least forward-declare the function in `cpp_sources`, and place the implementation in `cuda_sources`. Alternatively, we may need to include a header in both, but `load_inline` compiles `cpp_sources` as `main.cpp` separate from `cuda.cu`, and they get linked together. However, the generated `main.cpp` calls `torch::wrap_pybind_function` on the function name, which is defined in the CUDA compilation unit. It should be fine if the function is declared in `main.cpp` or known via a header. The previous error indicates `main.cpp` didn't see the declaration. To fix: Provide a `cpp_sources` string that contains a forward declaration of the function (as `torch::Tensor fused_forward(...)`) before the pybind module generation. But `load_inline` generates `main.cpp` internally; I think we can provide `cpp_sources` with the declaration and `cuda_sources` with the definition. According to PyTorch docs, `cpp_sources` is compiled as C++ separately but linked with cuda_sources. Alternatively, use `torch::Tensor fused_forward(...);` forward declaration in `cpp_sources`. Let's try that.

i < 16; ++i) {\n acc[i] = 0.0f;\n }\n\n int num_in_tiles = channels / TILE_C_IN;\n int x_stride_n = channels * seq_len;\n int x_stride_c = seq_len;\n int w_stride_oc = channels * 3;\n int w_stride_ic = 3;\n\n for (int tile_c_in = 0; tile_c_in < num_in_tiles; ++tile_c_in) {\n int c_in_start = tile_c_in * TILE_C_IN;\n int tid = ty * blockDim.x + tx;\n int total_loads = TILE_C_IN * 3 * TILE_L;\n\n for (int idx = tid; idx < total_loads; idx += blockDim.x * blockDim.y) {\n int ic = idx / (3 * TILE_L);\n int rem = idx % (3 * TILE_L);\n int k = rem / TILE_L;\n int t_local = rem % TILE_L;\n int global_t = t_start + t_local + (k - 1) * dilation;\n\n float val;\n if (global_t < 0 || global_t >= seq_len) {\n val = 0.0f;\n } else {\n int c = c_in_start + ic;\n int x_idx = n * x_stride_n + c * x_stride_c + global_t;\n val = x[x_idx];\n }\n x_shared[ic][k][t_local] = val;\n }\n __syncthreads();\n\n int global_ic_base = c_in_start;\n #pragma unroll\n for (int ic = 0; ic < TILE_C_IN; ++ic) {\n float xv0 = x_shared[ic][0][tx];\n float xv1 = x_shared[ic][1][tx];\n float xv2 = x_shared[ic][2][tx];\n int global_ic = global_ic_base + ic;\n\n #pragma unroll\n for (int i = 0; i < 16; ++i) {\n int oc = oc_base + i;\n float w0 = conv_weight[oc * w_stride_oc + global_ic * w_stride_ic + 0];\n float w1 = conv_weight[oc * w_stride_oc + global_ic * w_stride_ic + 1];\n float w2 = conv_weight[oc * w_stride_oc + global_ic * w_stride_ic + 2];\n acc[i] += w0 * xv0 + w1 * xv1 + w2 * xv2;\n }\n }\n __syncthreads();\n }\n\n #pragma unroll\n for (int i = 0; reasoning
The difference came from my kernel computing the dilated convolution differently from PyTorch's Conv1d with explicit padding=dilation. PyTorch's Conv1d uses padding=dilation, meaning it adds dilation zeros to both sides. The weight has size [256, 256, 3] (out_ch, in_ch, 3). For a dilated conv with dilation=128, padding=128, each output at index t is sum(weight[:, :, k] * input[:, :, t - (k-1)*dilation]) where the input includes padding. In my last check (kernel 3), I used a PyTorch reference with batch norm, activation and dilated conv, then my kernel applied the dilated conv to the pre-processed x. The difference could be from: 1. BatchNorm running vs training mode 2. The residual x used by my kernel should be the original x, not a version affected by in-place operations 3. That is exactly the tolerance. But wait, correctness check in tests uses 1e-4 for fp32. So that would be a clear mismatch. Let me think about the PyTorch Conv1d exact formula for padding. If input=x (after batch norm + ReLU) and padding=p, weight size=3, dilation=d: For output_t: sum_{k=0}^{2} weight[:, :, k] * x_pad[:, :, t + p - k*dilation] [assuming proper channel dims] Where x_pad is x padded with p zeros on both sides. In the standard formula, Conv1d output at time t uses input indices: t' = t + p - k * d, where k=0,1,2 for kernel size 3. Since p=d=128: - k=0: t' = t + 128 - k=1: t' = t - k=2: t' = t - 128 My kernel loads input at global_t for output position t_start+tx with k shifted by (k-1)*dilation: For tile of outputs centered around t, I loaded x at: t_start + t_local + (k-1)*dilation For shared mem indices: k=0 (first element): global_t = t + (-1)*dilation = t - d k=1: global_t = t k=2: global_t = t + d Then I compute: acc += w0*xv0 + w1*xv1 + w2*xv2 where xv0 corresponds to x at t-d, xv1=t, xv2=t+d. The difference between doing everything in one kernel vs. separate operations. In separate ops: 1. BN: (x - mean) / sqrt(var + eps) * gamma + beta 2. ReLU: max(0, x) 3. Conv: matmul + bias 4. Add: + residual In my kernel: 1. Load x, immediately apply BN+ReLU, store in shared 2. Accumulate conv 3. Add bias and residual, write output Mathematically, this is the exact same computation. Unless... there's some precision issue in PyTorch's BN. For instance, PyTorch might compute `(x - mean) * sqrt(var + eps)` in a fused way internally, or it might use different precision for intermediate results. Actually, there's another subtle issue. In PyTorch's BatchNorm, the formula actually involves a precomputed `scale` and `bias`: ```python scale = weight / sqrt(var + eps) bias = beta - mean * scale output = input * scale + bias ``` If I compute `val = (val - mean) * invstd` and then `val = val * weight + bias`, it's: `val = (val - mean) * invstd * weight + bias` which expands to `val * invstd * weight - mean * invstd * weight + bias`. If PyTorch computes `scale = weight * invstd` and `offset = bias - mean * scale`, then does `val * scale + offset`: `val * weight * invstd + bias - mean * weight * invstd` = `val * weight * invstd - mean * weight * invstd + bias` = `(val - mean) * weight * invstd + bias` This is mathematically the same! But due to floating-point order of operations, there could be slight differences. However, `(val - mean) * invstd * weight` vs `val * (invstd * weight) - mean * (invstd * weight)` - these are very similar. Actually, for numerical accuracy, the direct computation `(val - mean) * invstd * weight + bias` might actually be MORE accurate than precomputing scale and offset, because the latter can suffer from catastrophic cancellation in `bias - mean * scale` if they are close to each other. So: 0, pos0, pos1. - For t=127: sees 0, 127, 255. These are in subseq 0, 127, 127. Wait, 127 is in subseq 127 (127%128=127, 127//128=0). 255 is in subseq 127 (255%128=127, 255//128=1). That's: pad for pos-1 (since padding=1 means pos0 sees pad0), pos0, pos1. - For t=128: sees 0, 128, 256. 0 is in subseq0 pos0, 128 is in subseq0 pos1, 256 is in subseq0 pos2. So: pos0, pos1, pos2. This matches exactly! The boundary handling is the same because padding=1 on each subsequence corresponds to padding=dilation=128 on the original. So the reformulation is exact! Actually, `permute` followed by `reshape` can sometimes be done without copy if the strides are compatible, but `.permute(0, 2, 1, 3)` on a contiguous [N, C, g, l] tensor produces non-contiguous output, and the subsequent reshape to [N*g, C, l] requires the innermost dimensions to be contiguous. After permute, the innermost dimension l has stride 1, but C has stride l, and g has stride C*l. So `(N*g)*C*l` with strides `C*l, l, 1` for [N*g, C, l]... Let me check if reshape works. Original after view: [N, C, g, l], contiguous, strides = [C*g*l, g*l, l, 1] After permute(0, 2, 1, 3): [N, g, C, l], strides = [C*g*l, l, g*l, 1] To reshape to [N*g, C, l], we need the logical shape to be compatible with strides. The element at [N*g, C, l] with contiguous strides would be [C*l, l, 1]. Our actual strides for [N, g, C, l] are [C*g*l, l, g*l, 1]. If we merge N and g into the first dimension, we need the stride of the merged dimension to be the same as g's stride, which is l. But in the original, N's stride is C*g*l. When merging contiguous dimensions in reshape, the stride of the merged dimension should be the same as the last dimension's stride in the merge group. Here, [N, g] should have stride `g*l` (if g was last) or `l`? Actually, reshape can merge dimensions if the product of their sizes equals the product in the new shape, and the strides are monotonically decreasing. For [N, g, C, l] with strides [C*g*l, l, g*l, 1]: - Can we merge N and g? The product N*g should correspond to the first dim of new shape [N*g, C, l]. - The stride of merged dimension would be stride(g) = l. - This should work because C*g*l / g = C*l, and indeed N*g is the size. - More precisely, reshape allows merging dimensions d1, d2 if stride(d2) = stride(d1) * size(d1). Here, stride(g) = l, stride(N) = C*g*l, size(N) = N. Is l = C*g*l * N? No! That's not true. So reshape might need to copy the tensor to be contiguous. Let me verify with actual PyTorch: ```python x = torch.randn(2, 3, 4, 5) y = x.permute(0, 2, 1, 3) z = y.reshape(2*4, 3, 5) ``` This would raise an error or copy? To reshape to [8, 3, 5], the stride needs to be [15, 5, 1] for contiguous. But the actual strides of [2, 4, 3, 5] are [60, 5, 20, 1]. Can this be viewed as [8, 3, 5]? The element at [i, j, k] in [8, 3, 5] should be at offset i*15 + j*5 + k. But in [2, 4, 3, 5], the offset is a*60 + b*5 + c*20 + d. For a*4+b = i, c = j, d = k. So offset = (i//4)*60 + (i%4)*5 + j*20 + k = i*15 - (i%4)*15 + (i%4)*5 + j*20 + k. This is NOT equal to i*15 + j*5 + k unless 20 = 5, which is false. So reshape will copy. Let me verify with a small example. Suppose x has shape [1, 1, 6], conv has kernel=3, dilation=2, padding=2. Original output at t: - t=0: padded input at -2, 0, 2. In group 1 conv, pos 2 sees x[3], x[5], pad. Same values. The reformulation is EXACT for all positions when seq_len is divisible by dilation.
